                }

<script>
    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    <meta name="journal_id" content="41467"/>

    <meta name="dc.title" content="The genomic landscape of molecular responses to natural drought stress in Panicum hallii"/>

    <meta name="dc.source" content="Nature Communications 2018 9:1"/>

    <meta name="dc.format" content="text/html"/>

    <meta name="dc.publisher" content="Nature Publishing Group"/>

    <meta name="dc.date" content="2018-12-06"/>

    <meta name="dc.type" content="OriginalPaper"/>

    <meta name="dc.language" content="En"/>

    <meta name="dc.copyright" content="2018 The Author(s)"/>

    <meta name="dc.rightsAgent" content="journalpermissions@springernature.com"/>

    <meta name="dc.description" content="Environmental stress is a major driver of ecological community dynamics and agricultural productivity. This is especially true for soil water availability, because drought is the greatest abiotic inhibitor of worldwide crop yields. Here, we test the genetic basis of drought responses in the genetic model for C4 perennial grasses, Panicum hallii, through population genomics, field-scale gene-expression (eQTL) analysis, and comparison of two complete genomes. While gene expression networks are dominated by local cis-regulatory elements, we observe three genomic hotspots of unlinked trans-regulatory loci. These regulatory hubs are four times more drought responsive than the genome-wide average. Additionally, cis- and trans-regulatory networks are more likely to have opposing effects than expected under neutral evolution, supporting a strong influence of compensatory evolution and stabilizing selection. These results implicate trans-regulatory evolution as a driver of drought responses and demonstrate the potential for crop improvement in drought-prone regions through modification of gene regulatory networks. Drought is a major factor limiting crop productivity. Here, via eQTL analysis and comparative genomics, the authors show compensatory evolution between trans-regulatory loci and transcription factor binding sites that shape the drought response networks in the model C4 grass Panicum hallii."/>

            <div class="c-article-body">
                <section aria-labelledby="Abs1" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs1">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs1-content"><p>Environmental stress is a major driver of ecological community dynamics and agricultural productivity. This is especially true for soil water availability, because drought is the greatest abiotic inhibitor of worldwide crop yields. Here, we test the genetic basis of drought responses in the genetic model for C<sub>4</sub> perennial grasses, <i>Panicum hallii</i>, through population genomics, field-scale gene-expression (eQTL) analysis, and comparison of two complete genomes. While gene expression networks are dominated by local cis-regulatory elements, we observe three genomic hotspots of unlinked trans-regulatory loci. These regulatory hubs are four times more drought responsive than the genome-wide average. Additionally, cis- and trans-regulatory networks are more likely to have opposing effects than expected under neutral evolution, supporting a strong influence of compensatory evolution and stabilizing selection. These results implicate trans-regulatory evolution as a driver of drought responses and demonstrate the potential for crop improvement in drought-prone regions through modification of gene regulatory networks.</p></div></div></section>

                </noscript>

                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Introduction"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Introduction</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>Drought is the greatest abiotic determinant of agricultural yield<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Ciais, P. et al. Europe-wide reduction in primary productivity caused by the heat and drought in 2003. Nature 437, 529–533 (2005)." href="/articles/s41467-018-07669-x#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d12848e819">1</a></sup> and a key regulator of both ecological net productivity and carbon sequestration<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Hoover, D. L., Knapp, A. K. &amp; Smith, M. D. Resistance and resilience of a grassland ecosystem to climate extremes. Ecology 95, 2646–2656 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-018-07669-x#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d12848e823">2</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Wilcox, K. R. et al. Asymmetric responses of primary productivity to precipitation extremes: a synthesis of grassland precipitation manipulation experiments. Glob. Chang Biol. 23, 4376–4385 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-018-07669-x#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d12848e826">3</a></sup>. Unlike annual plant species, which can escape environmental stress through flowering time evolution, perennial plants must persist through periods of drought. While constitutive drought tolerance can lead to slower growth and lower yields, there is considerable genetic variation in the physiological nature and efficacy of facultative responses to soil moisture variation among plants<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Juenger, T. E. Natural variation and genetic constraints on drought tolerance. Curr. Opin. Plant Biol. 16, 274–281 (2013)." href="/articles/s41467-018-07669-x#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d12848e830">4</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Boyer, J. S. Plant productivity and environment. Science 218, 443–448 (1982)." href="/articles/s41467-018-07669-x#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d12848e833">5</a></sup>. Leveraging such genotype-by-environment interactions (GxE), especially in response to drought, is key to crop improvement.</p><p>Despite the central role that GxE plays in adaptation and plant productivity, the genetic basis of evolved differences in stress responses are poorly understood, except in a handful of laboratory model systems. However, it is clear that gene expression networks play particularly important roles in the evolution of physiological GxE as transcription factors and other regulatory elements are often environmentally induced<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Wagner, G. P. &amp; Lynch, V. J. The gene regulatory logic of transcription factor evolution. Trends Ecol. Evol. 23, 377–385 (2008)." href="/articles/s41467-018-07669-x#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d12848e840">6</a></sup>.</p><p>Regulatory elements fall into two main categories: distant trans-acting modifiers (e.g. transcription factors) and local cis-regulatory elements (e.g. promoter or coding sequence variants). Selection works most efficiently on traits that act in isolation; therefore, cis-elements, which typically regulate a single gene, may be particularly important in adaptive evolution<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Schaefke, B. et al. Inheritance of gene expression level and selective constraints on trans- and cis-regulatory changes in yeast. Mol. Biol. Evol. 30, 2121–2133 (2013)." href="/articles/s41467-018-07669-x#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d12848e847">7</a></sup>. Conversely, trans factors may cause correlated expression variation among many downstream genes, which should increase interference among loci and reduce the adaptive potential of global trans-regulatory evolution<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Keightley, P. D. &amp; Hill, W. G. Variation maintained in quantitative traits with mutation-selection balance: pleiotropic side-effects on fitness traits. Proc. Biol. Sci. 242, 95–100 (1990)." href="/articles/s41467-018-07669-x#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d12848e851">8</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Kondrashov, A. S. &amp; Turelli, M. Deleterious mutations, apparent stabilizing selection and the maintenance of quantitative variation. Genetics 132, 603–618 (1992)." href="/articles/s41467-018-07669-x#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d12848e854">9</a></sup>. To this end, many global regulatory elements evolve much more slowly than their target sequences<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Matsukura, S. et al. Comprehensive analysis of rice DREB2-type genes that encode transcription factors involved in the expression of abiotic stress-responsive genes. Mol. Genet. Genom. 283, 185–196 (2010)." href="#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d12848e858">10</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="DesMarais, D. L. et al. Physiological genomics of response to soil drying in diverse arabidopsis accessions. Plant Cell 24, 893–914 (2012)." href="#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d12848e858_1">11</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Mizoi, J., Shinozaki, K. &amp; Yamaguchi-Shinozaki, K. AP2/ERF family transcription factors in plant abiotic stress responses. Biochim. Biophys. Acta 1819, 86–96 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-018-07669-x#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d12848e861">12</a></sup>. Combined, cis- and trans-regulatory elements shape the gene expression landscape and form the basis of environmental stress responses. Therefore, defining the regulatory elements that lead to evolved differences in stress responses will improve our understanding of stress adaptation and the genetic basis of GxE.</p><p>To understand the genetic basis of drought stress tolerance in the context of perenniality, we have developed <i>Panicum hallii</i> as a genomic model for Panicoid grasses<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Lowry, D. B. et al. The genetics of divergence and reproductive isolation between ecotypes of Panicum hallii. New Phytol. 205, 402–414 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-018-07669-x#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d12848e871">13</a></sup>. The Panicoideae is a diverse subfamily of predominantly perennial warm-season grasses with efficient C<sub>4</sub> photosynthesis<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="SORENG, R. J. et al. A worldwide phylogenetic classification of the Poaceae (Gramineae). J. Sytematics Evol. 53, 117–137 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-018-07669-x#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d12848e877">14</a></sup>, which imparts drought-tolerance and some of the highest biomass production among plants<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Somerville, C., Youngs, H., Taylor, C., Davis, S. C. &amp; Long, S. P. Feedstocks for lignocellulosic biofuels. Science 329, 790–792 (2010)." href="/articles/s41467-018-07669-x#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d12848e881">15</a></sup>. Not surprisingly, the Panicoideae encompasses the most promising bioenergy feedstock crops, including switchgrass, <i>Sorghum</i>, big bluestem, <i>Miscanthus</i>, and sugar cane. The geographic distribution of <i>P. hallii</i> spans a massive moisture availability gradient: the lowland variety (<i>P. hallii var. filipes</i>; hereon <i>filipes</i>) inhabits riparian and coastal sites with &gt;100 cm of annual precipitation while upland (<i>P. hallii var. hallii</i>; hereon <i>hallii</i>) populations are found across southwestern North America<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Lowry, D. B., Purmal, C. T. &amp; Juenger, T. E. A population genetic transect of Panicum hallii (Poaceae). Am. J. Bot. 100, 592–601 (2013)." href="/articles/s41467-018-07669-x#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d12848e908">16</a></sup> in both desert (&lt;20 cm annual precipitation) and semi-arid habitats. The extensive physiological diversity<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Lowry, D. B. et al. The genetics of divergence and reproductive isolation between ecotypes of Panicum hallii. New Phytol. 205, 402–414 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-018-07669-x#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d12848e912">13</a></sup> of <i>P. hallii</i> and its close evolutionary relationship to key crops make it an ideal genetic model for biotechnology development in perennial biofuel feedstocks.</p><p>Here we present a set of experiments and analyses that dissect the genetic basis of <i>P. hallii</i> drought responses, connecting DNA sequence variation to leaf-level physiology. We first assess the demographic history and population structure of the upland and lowland varieties of <i>P. hallii</i>. To understand the scale of genomic divergence between varieties, we compare complete de novo genome assemblies and annotations for a single genotype of each variety. We then explore the genetic basis of evolved drought-responsive gene expression in an F<sub>2</sub> mapping population. Through eQTL mapping and comparative genomics, we demonstrate that trans-regulatory elements and transcription factor binding site evolution are key contributors to molecular drought responses in <i>P. hallii</i>. Combined, these results and genetic resources elevate <i>P. hallii</i> among the elite plant genomes, provide the genetic model for perennial feedstocks, and begin to decipher the complex drought-responsive genetic networks that have diverged between upland and lowland ecotypes.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Results"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Results</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">Demographic history of <i>P. hallii</i>
                        </h3><p>Habitat divergence between <i>filipes</i> and <i>hallii</i> is likely due to both adaptation and historical demographic processes associated with paleo-climatic change in North America. To explore the recent evolutionary history of these varieties, we deeply re-sequenced (35x median sequence coverage) 15 <i>filipes</i> and 78 <i>hallii</i> natural accessions (Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-07669-x#MOESM4">1</a>) spanning the USA geographic range of each variety (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-07669-x#Fig1">1a</a>). Demographic models (Supplementary Figure <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-07669-x#MOESM1">1</a>) indicated that <i>hallii</i> and <i>filipes</i> began evolutionary divergence &gt;1 M years before present (ybp). This conclusion was confirmed by a molecular-clock based estimate of divergence of 1.08 M ybp. Furthermore, pairwise cross-coalescence, a measure of the degree of genetic divergence between populations<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Schiffels, S. &amp; Durbin, R. Inferring human population size and separation history from multiple genome sequences. Nat. Genet. 46, 919–925 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-018-07669-x#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d12848e981">17</a></sup>, between any two <i>hallii</i> and <i>filipes</i> subpopulations (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-07669-x#Fig1">1b</a>) all declined below 0.25 at least 500k ypb (Supplementary Figure <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-07669-x#MOESM1">1</a>). These data indicate that the two <i>P. hallii</i> varieties have maintained substantial reproductive isolation over a period of intense climatic fluctuations, including at least the last two glacial-interglacial cycles.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Fig. 1"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 1</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-018-07669-x/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-018-07669-x/MediaObjects/41467_2018_7669_Fig1_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-018-07669-x/MediaObjects/41467_2018_7669_Fig1_HTML.png" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="549" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p>Genetic structure and geographic distribution of <i>P. hallii.</i> One individual was deeply re-sequenced from 94 locations across southwest USA (<b>a</b>). Filled points represent the geographic location of the population where each individual was collected. Point colors represent genetic subpopulation assignments from STRUCTURE. The map background is cropped from the annual precipitation (BIO12) 2.5-minute raster (<a href="http://worldclim.org/version2">http://worldclim.org/version2</a>) via a 10-minute United States of American state-boundary vector shapefile (<a href="http://naturalearthdata.com">http://naturalearthdata.com</a>). In addition to between-variety divergence (<i>filipes</i>: blue, <i>hallii</i>: red/orange/yellow), there was strong population structure within varieties (<b>b</b>); proportional subpopulation assignment is shown in pie charts at the branch tips. Branch lengths are proportional to the number of substitutions per variable site, except the root branch and the internal branch connecting varieties, which are labeled with the respective lengths; branches marked * have bootstrap support &gt;90%. Source data are provided as a Source Data file</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-018-07669-x/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Effective population size (<i>N</i><sub><i>e</i></sub>) of the largest two <i>hallii</i> subpopulations (inferred via genetic clustering, Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-07669-x#Fig1">1b</a>) expanded following reduction of cross coalescence until 20–100k ybp, while <i>N</i><sub><i>e</i></sub> of the largest <i>filipes</i> subpopulation remained relatively stable and ~50% smaller than any <i>hallii</i> subpopulations (Supplementary Figure <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-07669-x#MOESM1">2</a>; Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-07669-x#MOESM5">2</a>) over this period. However, <i>N</i><sub><i>e</i></sub> of all subpopulations have contracted over the last ~50k years. Combined, these demographic patterns suggest that the current range of <i>hallii</i> is similar to, or smaller than its historical distribution and is not purely the product of a recent population expansion. Instead, between-variety habitat differentiation is consistent with historical range expansion of <i>hallii</i> into arid regions of the North American southwest.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">Development and comparison of two complete de novo genomes</h3><p>To test for a genetic signal of drought-associated evolution, we developed complete de novo reference genomes for the basal <i>P. hallii</i> accession (<i>var. hallii HAL2</i>) and a representative <i>var. filipes</i> accession (<i>FIL2</i>) (Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-07669-x#Fig1">1</a>b, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-07669-x#Fig2">2a–d</a>). These Pacific Biosciences single molecule-based chromosomal assemblies contain 484.6 Mb (99.4% of assembled sequence in chromosomes, contig N/L50 = 15/8.3 Mb) and 508.0 Mb (94.8%, 117/1.1 Mb) of total <i>HAL2</i> and <i>FIL2</i> sequence, making them among the most complete plant genomes ever assembled (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-07669-x#MOESM1">Supplementary Notes</a> <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-07669-x#MOESM1">1</a>–<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-07669-x#MOESM1">2</a>; Supplementary Figure <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-07669-x#MOESM1">3</a>; Supplementary Tables <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-07669-x#MOESM1">1</a>–<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-07669-x#MOESM1">4</a>). Critically, these genomes exhibit near-perfect chromosome-scale synteny (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-07669-x#Fig2">2d</a>, Supplemental Note <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-07669-x#MOESM1">3</a>; Supplementary Figure <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-07669-x#MOESM1">4</a>, Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-07669-x#MOESM1">5</a>; Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-07669-x#MOESM6">3</a>). A total of 475 kb (&lt;0.1%) of the <i>HAL2</i> genomic assembly was not collinear with <i>FIL2</i>, including a 0.18 Mb translocation on Chr09 and a 0.295 Mb translocation between <i>HAL2</i> Chr02 and <i>FIL2</i> Chr04 (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-07669-x#MOESM1">5</a>). Additionally, a duplication was present on the proximate telomere and adjacent 2.22 Mb region of Chr03 and Chr08 in both genomes. This duplication is also present in <i>Sorghum bicolor</i> (Supplementary Figure <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-07669-x#MOESM1">4</a>), indicating that it is ancient and ancestral to <i>Panicum</i>.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Fig. 2"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 2</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-018-07669-x/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-018-07669-x/MediaObjects/41467_2018_7669_Fig2_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-018-07669-x/MediaObjects/41467_2018_7669_Fig2_HTML.png" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="722" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p>Synteny and structure of the <i>P. hallii</i> genome. The <i>P. hallii</i> genomes are characterized by highly genic chromosome arms and non-recombinant pericentromeric regions. The physical size and orientation of each chromosome (minor ticks at 10 Mb intervals) are plotted along the outer-most track (<b>a</b>). Gene models and repetitive sequences were annotated for both assemblies - the proportional representation (in 5 Mb overlapping windows) of five annotation categories, and unannotated intergenic sequence (white), are plotted in the second track (<b>b</b>). A heatmap of recombination rate (cM/Mb) is shown in the third track (<b>c</b>), where the darkest red represents &gt;4% chance of a crossover event per F<sub>2</sub> individual per Mb. The interior links (<b>d</b>) connect 72 syntenic blocks, which cover 369.2 Mb (75.8%) of the total <i>HAL2</i> assembly and 254 Mb (84.5%) of the chromosome arms. Source data are provided as a Source Data file</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-018-07669-x/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>It is possible to leverage such extensive synteny to infer sequence evolution and presence-absence variation (PAV) among orthologous clusters of genes. This analysis was performed via our GENESPACE pipeline (Supplementary Note <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-07669-x#MOESM1">3</a>), which employs a multi-species orthologous gene network construction approach constrained within collinear sequence blocks. GENESPACE allows for construction of outgroup-rooted gene networks and sequence alignments within duplicated (e.g. proximate Chr03 and Chr08) and single-copy regions. Critically, by informing orthology networks with outgroup sequences, we can determine whether gene annotations without orthologous sequences between <i>HAL2</i> and <i>FIL2</i> are derived or lost in each genome. Overall, the majority of high-confidence gene models (41,017 genes; 80.1%) existed as either single copy (‘1:1’) orthogroups or orthogroups with two or more members in at least one genome (‘1:2 + ’, ‘2 + :1’ or ‘2 + :2 + ’; Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-07669-x#Tab1">1</a>; Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-07669-x#MOESM7">4</a>). However, 10,176 genes (19.9% of all gene models) lacked annotated orthologous sequence in either <i>HAL2</i> or <i>FIL2</i> (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-07669-x#Tab1">1</a>; Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-07669-x#MOESM8">5</a>). These PAV genes may be the product of annotation support thresholding, pseudogenization or true sequence deletion in one genome.</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-1"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab1" data-test="table-caption">Table 1 Summary of CDS orthology between <i>HAL2</i> and <i>FIL2</i></b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/s41467-018-07669-x/tables/1"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>To evaluate the evolution of these PAV genes, we compared DNA variation between annotated coding DNA sequence (CDS) of present genes with the unannotated assembly sequence of syntenic orthologous regions in the other genome. The majority of PAV genes were private to either <i>HAL2</i> or <i>FIL2</i> and never had orthologous sequences in any outgroups (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-07669-x#Tab1">1</a>, Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-07669-x#MOESM8">5</a>). Other studies find that such private genes are generally of low quality<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Grover, C. E. et al. Comparative genomics of an unusual biogeographic disjunction in the cotton tribe (Gossypieae) yields insights into genome downsizing. Genome Biol. Evol. 9, 3328–3344 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-018-07669-x#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d12848e1710">18</a></sup> and may not represent novel derived CDS, but instead are simply due to weak gene evidence that only survives thresholding in a single genome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="van Velzen, R. et al. Comparative genomics of the nonlegume Parasponia reveals insights into evolution of nitrogen-fixing rhizobium symbioses. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 115, E4700–E4709 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-018-07669-x#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d12848e1715">19</a></sup>. Indeed, private genes were 9.6x more likely to be low confidence, with low homology or transcript support, than 1:1 orthogroup genes.</p><p>To understand sequence variation underlying the remaining PAV genes, which also had at least one outgroup sequence in the network, we categorized the degree of sequence variation between CDS and un-annotated DNA sequence of the alternative genome into un-annotated (&gt;90% sequence identity), pseudogenized (90–10% identity), and deleted ( &lt; 10% identity) groups (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-07669-x#Tab1">1</a>). Like the private gene models, the 296 un-annotated gene models were much more likely to be low confidence than genes with 1:1 orthologs (Fisher’s test odds ratio = 11.0 × , <i>P</i> &lt; 1 × 10<sup>–16</sup>, Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-07669-x#Tab1">1</a>, Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-07669-x#MOESM9">6</a>). Combined the un-annotated and private PAV genes are very likely to be the product of annotation support thresholding and not true sequence deletion or pseudogenization. The remaining 454 pseudogenized and deleted gene models were 2.49x more likely to be high confidence than the private and un-annotated genes (Fisher’s test <i>P</i> &lt; 1 × 10<sup>−16</sup>), indicating that pseudogenized and deleted genes may represent biologically relevant presence-absence variation.</p><p>To confirm the quantitative genetic effects of the observed PAV, we counted expression of each unique allele across the two genomes in leaf tissue of an F<sub>2</sub> population (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-07669-x#Tab1">1</a>). These results largely mirror inference based on gene model confidence: private and un-annotated genes had weak expression, while pseudogenized and deleted gene models showed similar levels of expression as those within orthologous gene networks. Combined, the relative rarity of such PAV indicates that small insertion/deletions (INDELs) and single-nucleotide polymorphisms (SNPs), and not large sequence deletions, represent the vast majority of molecular evolution between <i>HAL2</i> and <i>FIL2</i>. Furthermore, protein coding DNA sequence (CDS) gain and loss is not common despite the &gt;1 My divergence time between <i>HAL2</i> and <i>FIL2</i>. Such conserved gene content and synteny despite significant sequence divergence (mean <i>π</i> = 0.0195) makes <i>P. hallii</i> an ideal system to test hypotheses about sequence evolution, selection, and molecular adaptation.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">Genetic mapping to dissect regulatory network evolution</h3><p>To map the genetic basis of physiological divergence between varieties, we conducted a large-scale field drought experiment where 25 <i>HAL2</i> and 34 <i>FIL2</i> replicates and 243 <i>FIL2</i>x<i>HAL2</i> F<sub>2</sub> genotypes were subjected to a month-long natural drought. Half of the plants were watered 24 h prior to harvest (recovery treatment), while the remainder were harvested under existing drought conditions. At the physiological scale, midday leaf water potential (a measure of plant water status) was more responsive to re-watering among <i>HAL2</i> than <i>FIL2</i> plants (<i>χ</i><sup>2</sup><sub>df = 1</sub> <i>=</i> 4.01, <i>P</i> = 0.045, Supplementary Figure <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-07669-x#MOESM1">5</a>). This suggests that the arid-adapted <i>hallii</i> was more sensitive to temporally variable water resources, a trait that is likely advantageous in desert ecosystems marked by drought but punctuated by brief periods of high soil moisture<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Roy, J. &amp; Mooney, H. A. Physiological adaptation and plasticity to water stress of coastal and desert populations of Heliotropium curassavicum L. Oecologia 52, 370–375 (1982)." href="/articles/s41467-018-07669-x#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d12848e1817">20</a></sup>.</p><p>Physiological responses like leaf water potential are driven in part by the evolution of gene expression regulatory sequences<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Feder, M. E. &amp; Hofmann, G. E. Heat-shock proteins, molecular chaperones, and the stress response: evolutionary and ecological physiology. Annu. Rev. Physiol. 61, 243–282 (1999)." href="/articles/s41467-018-07669-x#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d12848e1824">21</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Rengel, D. et al. A gene-phenotype network based on genetic variability for drought responses reveals key physiological processes in controlled and natural environments. PLoS One 7, e45249 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-018-07669-x#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d12848e1827">22</a></sup>. To dissect the genetic basis of regulatory network divergence between <i>hallii</i> and <i>filipes</i>, we assayed total RNA abundance of the F<sub>2</sub> population (Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-07669-x#MOESM10">7</a>), constructed a genetic map (Supplementary Figure <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-07669-x#MOESM1">6</a>), and subsequently conducted gene expression quantitative trait locus (eQTL) mapping. To determine significance of constitutive (QTL) and treatment-responsive (QTL*E) QTL for the expression phenotype of each gene, we compared NULL (expression ~E; no QTL, only drought treatment), additive (expression ~QTL + E), and full (expression ~QTL + E + QTL*E) QTL models via likelihood ratio tests.</p><p>While PAV and multi-copy orthogroups are important aspects of evolution between <i>HAL2</i> and <i>FIL2</i>, regulatory network inference of these genes is obscured by different copy numbers of the focal gene. For example, some F<sub>2</sub> genotypes will simply lack a PAV gene model, which would bias inference of the strength and presence of eQTL. Therefore, we limited our eQTL analysis to only those high-confidence genes (which have both homology and transcript evidence) with one-to-one reciprocal best hit (RBH) orthologs between the two genomes. This test allowed us to count the total expression of 21,227 putative RBH orthologs (Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-07669-x#MOESM6">3</a>) by summing allele-specific and shared transcript counts; 80.4% (17,061) of these had a mean of &gt;1 raw count across the F<sub>2</sub> population. The voom<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Ritchie, M. E. et al. limma powers differential expression analyses for RNA-sequencing and microarray studies. Nucleic Acids Res. 43, e47–e47 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-018-07669-x#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d12848e1863">23</a></sup>-normalized transcript counts from these genes were used as the independent phenotypic variables in our eQTL analysis. Combined, we found significant eQTL among 58% of the 17,061 genes with RBH orthologs between <i>HAL2</i> and <i>FIL2</i>.</p><p>The genetic architecture of regulatory variants can have profound consequences on the evolutionary processes that produce heritable genetic diversity<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Schaefke, B. et al. Inheritance of gene expression level and selective constraints on trans- and cis-regulatory changes in yeast. Mol. Biol. Evol. 30, 2121–2133 (2013)." href="/articles/s41467-018-07669-x#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d12848e1876">7</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Emerson, J. J. et al. Natural selection on cis and trans regulation in yeasts. Genome Res. 20, 826–836 (2010)." href="/articles/s41467-018-07669-x#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d12848e1879">24</a></sup>. Cis-elements in coding or promoter regions typically regulate the expression of a single, physically linked gene. In contrast, trans-elements, like transcription factors, can have global regulatory effects, which may constrain adaptation by increasing the likelihood of interference and limiting the efficacy of selection<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Keightley, P. D. &amp; Hill, W. G. Variation maintained in quantitative traits with mutation-selection balance: pleiotropic side-effects on fitness traits. Proc. Biol. Sci. 242, 95–100 (1990)." href="/articles/s41467-018-07669-x#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d12848e1883">8</a></sup>. Due to the potential antagonistic effects of trans-regulatory elements, we expected drought-adaptive regulatory networks in <i>P. hallii</i> to be dominated by cis variants. Indeed, the 9,088 significant cis-eQTL on average explained 30.2% of total gene expression variation while the 1,314 trans eQTL explained just 7.4% (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-07669-x#Tab2">2</a>).</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-2"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab2" data-test="table-caption">Table 2 Summary of cis- and trans-eQTL effects</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/s41467-018-07669-x/tables/2"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>To develop candidate sequence variants for cis-regulatory QTL, we conducted coding (Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-07669-x#MOESM12">9</a>) and regulatory sequence (Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-07669-x#MOESM13">10</a>) alignments of RBH orthologs between the <i>HAL2</i> and <i>FIL2</i> genomes. Cis-regulated genes were significantly more likely to have accumulated non-synonymous and other coding variants than genes without QTL (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-07669-x#Tab2">2</a>). This enrichment of potentially functional variants was much stronger within promoter regions, where genes with cis-eQTL were 1.5x more likely to have evolved differences in transcription factor binding affinity (TFBA) than genes without eQTL (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-07669-x#Tab2">2</a>). While it is likely that genes with differential expression are generally subject to weaker evolutionary constraint than those without evolved expression variation, these results show that cis-eQTL are generally driven by proximate regulatory loci, and not by genetically linked, but physically distant, regulatory loci.</p><p>Genes with only trans-eQTL are, by definition, not differentially regulated by local sequence variants. Consistent with such non-local effects, genes regulated by only trans-eQTL had slightly more conserved CDS (Fisher’s test odds = 1.2x more conserved, <i>P</i> = 0.08) and similarly conserved TFBA (Fisher’s test odds = 1.08 × , <i>P</i> &gt; 0.1) regions as genes without eQTL. Furthermore, since trans-regulated loci are targets of transcriptional regulatory elements, we expected transcription factor binding sites to be very conserved among genes with only trans-eQTL. Indeed, such genes had 1.45x greater transcription factor binding site sequence conservation (Fisher’s test <i>P</i> &lt; 1 × 10<sup>−6</sup>) than genes with cis-eQTL, but less significantly conserved CDS regions (Fisher’s test odds = 1.32 × , <i>P</i> = 0.003, Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-07669-x#Tab2">2</a>). These patterns were even more significant among genes with GxE QTL (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-07669-x#Tab2">2</a>). These results suggest that non-synonymous variants are less responsible for the evolution of expression regulation than promoter sequences and demonstrate sequence conservation of trans-acting transcription factors binding sites.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec6">Exploring the causes of trans-eQTL hotspots</h3><p>Differential adaptation across habitats is an example of a genotype-by-environment interaction (GxE) for fitness<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Juenger, T. E. Natural variation and genetic constraints on drought tolerance. Curr. Opin. Plant Biol. 16, 274–281 (2013)." href="/articles/s41467-018-07669-x#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d12848e2314">4</a></sup>. Therefore, loci that contribute to adaptation may be disproportionately environmentally responsive and exhibit trade-offs<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Ågren, J., Oakley, C. G., McKay, J. K., Lovell, J. T. &amp; Schemske, D. W. Genetic mapping of adaptation reveals fitness tradeoffs in Arabidopsis thaliana. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 110, 21077–21082 (2013)." href="/articles/s41467-018-07669-x#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d12848e2318">25</a></sup>. Despite their global rarity, trans-eQTL were more than four times as likely as cis-eQTL to have significant GxE effects (Fisher’s test odds = 4.40, <i>P</i> &lt; 1 × 10<sup>−16</sup>, Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-07669-x#Tab2">2</a>), indicating a potentially adaptive role of trans-regulatory evolution between <i>P. hallii</i> varieties. Furthermore, there were three genomic hotspots where trans-eQTL were more common than cis-eQTL, which represented 3.1% of the physical genome sequence but 40.4% of all trans-eQTL (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-07669-x#Fig3">3a–b</a>, Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-07669-x#Tab3">3</a>). Combined, these hotspots were responsible for 3.84 × (Fisher’s test <i>P</i> &lt; 1 × 10<sup>−16</sup>) more GxE effects than all trans-eQTL outside this interval.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Fig. 3"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 3</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-018-07669-x/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-018-07669-x/MediaObjects/41467_2018_7669_Fig3_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-018-07669-x/MediaObjects/41467_2018_7669_Fig3_HTML.png" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="1146" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p>Distribution and effect of trans-eQTL. The position of each cis (on diagonal) and trans-eQTL (off diagonal) and the location of the proximate marker to the physical gene position are plotted (<b>a</b>). Points and line segments are colored green/black to distinguish adjacent chromosomes. The density of trans-eQTL/cM along sliding windows were scored across the genome (<b>b</b>). The three strongest peaks (hotspots) are labeled (*). The allelic effects of the *3a trans-eQTL are plotted as heatmaps, where red colors indicate higher gene-scaled expression (<b>c</b>). The color scale of the heatmap corresponds to the scaled allelic effects of the *3a hotspot, where increasing blue and red color intensity indicate stronger negative and positive allelic effects respectively. White cells have a scaled allelic effect of zero. We tested for biases of compensatory or reinforcing evolution between cis and trans eQTL. Overall, there was significant biases towards antagonistic effects, however this bias was much stronger in the drought than recovery treatment (<b>d</b>). Source data for panels <b>a</b> and <b>d</b> are found in Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-07669-x#MOESM1">8</a>. Source data for panels <b>b</b>, <b>c</b> are provided as a Source Data file</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-018-07669-x/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-3"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab3" data-test="table-caption">Table 3 Summary of direction of effects for genes with both cis- and trans-eQTL</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/s41467-018-07669-x/tables/3"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>It is important to note that the eQTL hotspots we identified may not be caused by regulatory element evolution, but instead could be driven by the presence of a large physiological QTL that altered plant water status and caused downstream gene expression variation of many water status-responsive genes. To test this hypothesis, we collected leaf water potential (LWP), which is the best available field-scale proxy for plant water status<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Jones, H. G. Monitoring plant and soil water status: established and novel methods revisited and their relevance to studies of drought tolerance. J. Exp. Bot. 58, 119–130 (2007)." href="/articles/s41467-018-07669-x#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d12848e2786">26</a></sup>, from the entire F<sub>2</sub> population at both pre-dawn and midday at the same time as RNA sampling. There were no significant QTL (lowest empirically-derived <i>P</i>-value = 0.27, Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-07669-x#MOESM1">6</a>) on any chromosome for either sampling period, indicating that genetic variation in plant water status does not in turn drive the physical clustering of trans-eQTL. Instead, the trans-eQTL hotspots and their significantly elevated environmental sensitivity suggest that pleiotropic mutations in transcription factors may be a major source of regulatory variation.</p><p>We searched for candidate genes by investigating the distribution of allelic effects among QTL mapping to each hotspot<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Tian, J. et al. The dissection of expression quantitative trait locus hotspots. Genetics 202, 1563–1574 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-018-07669-x#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d12848e2801">27</a></sup>. For example, expression of all but two genes regulated by trans-eQTL in the *3a primary hotspot were driven by plasticity of the <i>FIL2</i>, but not the <i>HAL2</i> allele (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-07669-x#Fig3">3c</a>). Within this 964 kb interval, three genes had significant cis-eQTL (Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-07669-x#MOESM11">8</a>) and elevated non-synonymous substitution rates (Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-07669-x#MOESM12">9</a>). The most promising candidate, <i>ABO3</i> (<i>P. hallii</i> ortholog of <i>A. thaliana AT1G66600</i> - <i>ABA Overly Sensitive 3</i>, <i>PhHAL.3G069100</i>/<i>Pahal.3G071300</i>), is a well-documented drought-responsive WRKY transcription factor. Knockout alleles of <i>ABO3</i> in <i>A. thaliana</i> induce extreme sensitivity to drought<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Ren, X. et al. ABO3, a WRKY transcription factor, mediates plant responses to abscisic acid and drought tolerance in Arabidopsis. Plant J. 63, 417–429 (2010)." href="/articles/s41467-018-07669-x#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d12848e2846">28</a></sup>. RT-qPCR revealed that the <i>FIL2</i> ABO3 allele is 27.7x downregulated relative to the <i>HAL2</i> allele across both well-watered and drought conditions (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-07669-x#MOESM1">7</a>). The elevated drought-response caused by the weak <i>FIL2</i> allele is consistent with the extreme environmental sensitivity of non-functional <i>ABO3</i> alleles in <i>A. thaliana</i>. It is important to note that eQTL analysis cannot definitively identify the causal loci driving trans-eQTL hotspots but instead provides candidates for future validation. However, the observed trans-eQTL colocalization clearly demonstrates the central role of global regulatory elements in evolved molecular drought responses.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec7">Signatures of selection among cis–trans regulatory networks</h3><p>Elevated GxE among trans-eQTL, particularly within the hotspots, indicates possible selection on gene expression plasticity. To test the hypothesis of non-neutral regulatory network evolution, we examined the QTL effect distribution among the 684 genes with both cis- and trans-eQTL (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-07669-x#Tab3">3</a>). Under a neutral model, cis- and trans-regulatory elements should evolve independently, which produces a random distribution of trans- relative to cis-effect directionality (up/down regulated)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Fraser, H. B., Moses, A. M. &amp; Schadt, E. E. Evidence for widespread adaptive evolution of gene expression in budding yeast. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 107, 2977–2982 (2010)." href="/articles/s41467-018-07669-x#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d12848e2881">29</a></sup>. However, directional and stabilizing selection produce correlated shifts among regulatory loci<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Fraser, H. B., Moses, A. M. &amp; Schadt, E. E. Evidence for widespread adaptive evolution of gene expression in budding yeast. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 107, 2977–2982 (2010)." href="/articles/s41467-018-07669-x#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d12848e2885">29</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Naranjo, S. et al. Dissecting the Genetic Basis of a Complex cis-Regulatory Adaptation. PLoS Genet 11, e1005751 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-018-07669-x#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d12848e2888">30</a></sup>. For example, if directional selection favors up-regulation of a gene, adaptive evolution of both cis- and trans-regulatory loci would increase expression; such positive correlations among loci are known as reinforcing QTL effects. Alternatively, if stabilizing selection favors maintenance of ancestral expression levels, non-adaptive evolution of increased expression may be compensated for by selection for regulatory repression at another locus. Such compensatory evolution promotes antagonistic (negatively correlated) cis–trans regulatory networks.</p><p>We observed a significant genome-wide bias towards antagonistic effects (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-07669-x#Fig3">3d</a>, Fisher’s test odds = 1.3, <i>P</i> = 0.015) and a very strong signal in the drought treatment, where allelic effects were greater than two times more likely to be antagonistic than under neutral expectations (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-07669-x#Fig3">3d</a>, Fisher’s test odds = 2.15, <i>P</i> &lt; 1 × 10<sup>−12</sup>). However, the relative strength of reinforcing and antagonistic effects was highly dependent on the position of the trans-eQTL (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-07669-x#Tab3">3</a>). For example, the *3a hotspot was enriched in reinforcing effects relative to all other trans-eQTL; however, the proportion of reinforcing effects at *3a was not significantly different than the neutral expectation of 1:1 (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-07669-x#Tab3">3</a>). In contrast, the *3b hotspot was disproportionately represented by genes with antagonistic cis-trans effects in both treatments relative to the background and neutral expectation (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-07669-x#Tab3">3</a>). These results indicate a genome-wide prevalence of antagonistic effects, which is typical of stabilizing selection on expression regulation.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec8">Validating the physiological effects of a trans-eQTL hotspot</h3><p>Given the large number of genes regulated and the elevated rate of GxE, the trans-eQTL hotspots clearly drive variation in molecular responses within our drought-recovery experiment. In switchgrass, regulatory responses depended on the intensity and duration of drought<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Lovell, J. T. et al. Promises and challenges of eco-physiological genomics in the field: tests of drought responses in switchgrass. Plant Physiol. 172, 734–748 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-018-07669-x#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d12848e2927">31</a></sup>, and our drought experiment is only a subsample of possible drought conditions. Therefore, we sought to validate the effects of the trans-eQTL hotspots in a broader context by exploring drought responses in a recombinant inbred line (RIL) population, derived from the F<sub>2</sub> used in the eQTL study (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-07669-x#MOESM1">8</a>). We assayed leaf relative water content (RWC) and chlorophyll content (SPAD) and conducted allelic contrasts at the proximate markers to each trans-eQTL hotspot peak position. Since RILs represent a random sample of genetic backgrounds, this analysis permits inference of causality when testing the effects of allelic differences at a locus.</p><p>Allelic variation at the *3b hotspot never significantly affected any treatment-trait combination, and the *7 hotspot had only one significant association (RWC under drought, Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-07669-x#MOESM1">8</a>). However, variation at the *3a hotspot marginally affected RWC plasticity (<i>t</i> = −1.95, <i>P</i> = 0.052), RWC in the drought treatment (<i>t</i> = 1.55, <i>P</i> = 0.081), and significantly affected SPAD in both recovery (<i>t</i> = 2.42, <i>P</i> = 0.016) and drought treatments (<i>t</i> = 2.02, <i>P</i> = 0.044). Individually, each of these effects are not highly significant. Yet, when taken together we observe strong support for functional effects of allelic variation at the *3a hotspot with a Fisher’s combined test (<i>χ</i><sup>2</sup><sub>df=12</sub> = 27.3, <i>P</i> = 0.0068, Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-07669-x#MOESM1">8</a>). Combined, the allelic effects at the *3a hotspot are consistent with those of ABO3 (lower plasticity, higher constitutive RWC and SPAD in <i>HAL2</i>) and demonstrate the predictive potential and multi-environment effect of trans-acting transcriptional regulatory elements.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec9" data-title="Discussion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec9-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec9">Discussion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec9-content"><p>The intersection of physiology, quantitative genetics, and whole genome sequencing holds great promise for understanding the complex interaction between genetic variation and the environment<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Lovell, J. T. et al. Promises and challenges of eco-physiological genomics in the field: tests of drought responses in switchgrass. Plant Physiol. 172, 734–748 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-018-07669-x#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d12848e2991">31</a></sup>. Using de novo whole genome assembly, large-scale field experimentation, and physiological genomic techniques in <i>P. hallii</i>, we were able to precisely map global regulatory loci and infer candidate variants. These experimental resources allow us to better infer the genetic basis of complex traits in plant adaptation, predict responses to current and future climatic stress, and develop a strategy for drought-responsive biotechnology in biofuel breeding programs.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec10" data-title="Methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec10-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec10">Methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec10-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec11">Genome sequencing assembly and annotation</h3><p>We sequenced the <i>Panicum hallii var. hallii</i> and <i>var. filipes</i> genotypes <i>HAL2</i> and <i>FIL2</i> using a whole genome shotgun strategy and standard sequencing protocols. Sequencing was conducted on both Illumina (HISeq) and Pacific Biosciences (SEQUEL) platforms at the Department of Energy Joint Genome Institute (JGI, Walnut Creek, CA, USA) and the HudsonAlpha Institute for Biotechnology (Huntsville AL, USA).</p><p>Sequencing effort for <i>HAL2</i> (Supplementary Note <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-07669-x#MOESM1">1</a>) included one 800 bp insert 2 × 250 fragment library (150 × ) and a total of 89.5x of PACBIO reads (average subread length 11.8 kb). <i>FIL2</i> sequencing was similar (Supplementary Note <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-07669-x#MOESM1">1</a>), with one 500 bp insert 2 × 150 fragment library (100 × ) and a total of 95.87x of Pacific Biosciences reads (average subread length 9.6 kb). Both the <i>FIL2</i> and <i>HAL2</i> assemblies were performed using MECAT<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Xiao, C.-L. et al. MECAT: fast mapping, error correction, and de novo assembly for single-molecule sequencing reads. Nat. Methods 14, 1072 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-018-07669-x#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d12848e3044">32</a></sup> and polished using QUIVER<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Chin, C.-S. et al. Nonhybrid, finished microbial genome assemblies from long-read SMRT sequencing data. Nat. Methods 10, 563–569 (2013)." href="/articles/s41467-018-07669-x#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d12848e3048">33</a></sup>.</p><p>We built a 325,613-marker map from shallow resequencing of the RIL population<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Khasanova, A. et al. The genetic architecture of shoot and root trait divergence between upland and lowland ecotypes of a perennial grass. Preprint at &#xA;                    https://doi.org/10.1101/301531&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-018-07669-x#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d12848e3055">34</a></sup>. This marker order was used to identify mis-joins in the assembly. A total of 115 mis-joins were identified in the <i>FIL2</i> assembly and only 1 mis-join in <i>HAL2</i>. Scaffolds were then oriented, ordered, and joined together. A total of 1246 (<i>FIL2</i>) and 119 (<i>HAL2</i>) joins were applied to the broken assemblies to form the final releases consisting of 9 chromosomes each. Short redundant sequence from contig ends were aligned to one another and collapsed when appropriate. A total of 18 (<i>HAL2</i>) and 450 (<i>FIL2</i>) repeated adjacent contig pairs were identified and collapsed. For the <i>FIL2</i> release, a set of 30,315 (430.3 Mb) targeted clone sequences (Illumina), along with a set of 704,618 (3.74 Gb, 6.9x coverage) MOLECULO reads were used to patch 60 sequence gaps. Finally, Illumina reads were employed to correct any remaining MECAT consensus calling errors (e.g. homozygous SNPs and INDELs).</p><p>PERTRAN (Supplementary Note <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-07669-x#MOESM1">2</a>) and PASA<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Haas, B. J. Improving the Arabidopsis genome annotation using maximal transcript alignment assemblies. Nucleic Acids Res. 31, 5654–5666 (2003)." href="/articles/s41467-018-07669-x#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d12848e3087">35</a></sup> were used to produce 92,211 (<i>HAL2</i>) and 111,325 (<i>FIL2</i>) transcript assemblies from ~1100 M (<i>HAL2</i>) and ~1200 M (<i>FIL2</i>) 2 × 150 paired-end Illumina RNA-seq reads. Repetitive DNA elements were identified de novo with repeatModeler<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Smit, A. &amp; Hubley, R. RepeatModeler Open-1.0. Repeat Masker Website (2010)." href="/articles/s41467-018-07669-x#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d12848e3104">36</a></sup>. For mapping and genome comparisons, we soft-masked the genomes using repeatMasker<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Smit, A. F., Hubley, R. &amp; Green, P. RepeatMasker. (1996)." href="/articles/s41467-018-07669-x#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d12848e3108">37</a></sup>, with ancestral repeats from RepBase and the repeat annotations from repeatModeler output. Loci were determined by transcript assembly alignments and/or EXONERATE (<a href="https://github.com/nathanweeks/exonerate">https://github.com/nathanweeks/exonerate</a>) alignments of proteins from <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i>, soybean, Kitaake rice, sorghum, foxtail millet, <i>Brachypodium distachyon</i>, grape and Swiss-Prot proteomes. Gene models were predicted by homology-based methods, FGENESH + /_EST<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Salamov, A. A. Ab initio gene finding in Drosophila genomic DNA. Genome Res. 10, 516–522 (2000)." href="/articles/s41467-018-07669-x#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d12848e3126">38</a></sup> GenomeScan<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Yeh, R. F., Lim, L. P. &amp; Burge, C. B. Computational inference of homologous gene structures in the human genome. Genome Res. 11, 803–816 (2001)." href="/articles/s41467-018-07669-x#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d12848e3130">39</a></sup>, and AUGUSTUS via BRAKER1<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Hoff, K. J., Lange, S., Lomsadze, A., Borodovsky, M. &amp; Stanke, M. BRAKER1: unsupervised RNA-Seq-based genome annotation with GeneMark-ET and AUGUSTUS: table 1. Bioinformatics 32, 767–769 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-018-07669-x#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d12848e3134">40</a></sup>. The best-scoring predictions for each locus were selected using EST and protein support and penalized if overlapped with repeats. PASA was employed to add UTRs, splicing corrections, and alternative transcripts. High-confidence transcripts were called for loci with BLASTP Cscores &gt;0.5, EST coverage, and &lt;20% of CDS overlapping with repeats (if &gt;20% overlap with repeats, only loci with Cscores &gt;0.9 and homology coverage &gt;0.70 were retained). Gene models with &gt;30% TE domains (Pfam) were also culled. Finally, gene models with a short single exon (&lt;300 BP CDS), without protein domain or with weak expression evidence were removed.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec12">Comparative genomics</h3><p>We used comparative genomic approaches to accomplish the following goals: (1) identify orthologous pairs of genes, (2) define the scale and causes of presence absence variation among gene annotations, and (3) understand the scale of synteny between the <i>HAL2</i> and <i>FIL2</i> genomes. Given these goals and the highly-repetitive and less conserved intergenic regions in plant genomes, we used a gene-level approach to whole-genome alignments. We ignored regions that were not in proximity to annotated gene models.</p><p>The GENESPACE pipeline (Supplementary Note <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-07669-x#MOESM1">3</a>) is applied to a set of de novo genomic assemblies and annotations. In short, GENESPACE conducts standard inference of orthology using the orthofinder<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Emms, D. M. &amp; Kelly, S. OrthoFinder: solving fundamental biases in whole genome comparisons dramatically improves orthogroup inference accuracy. Genome Biol. 16, E9 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-018-07669-x#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d12848e3158">41</a></sup> program but limits the search within known colinear (syntenic) blocks, generated by the multiple-collinearity inference program MCScanX<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Wang, Y. et al. MCScanX: a toolkit for detection and evolutionary analysis of gene synteny and collinearity. Nucleic Acids Res. 40, e49–e49 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-018-07669-x#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d12848e3162">42</a></sup>. This allows for the inference of orthology in duplicated chromosomal regions, as these appear as multiple distinct blocks in the alignments. In addition to pairwise peptide–peptide searches for orthologous gene groups, GENESPACE also conducts alignments against un-annotated genomic sequences (via BLAT<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Kent, W. J. BLAT--the BLAST-like alignment tool. Genome Res. 12, 656–664 (2002)." href="/articles/s41467-018-07669-x#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d12848e3166">43</a></sup> and EXONERATE) to discover the sequence identity of pseudogenized or otherwise un-annotated loci. The pipeline outputs alignments and some general sequence-divergence statistics for all orthogroup sequences among all genomes considered.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec13">F<sub>2</sub> RNA sequencing and analysis</h3><p><i>HAL2</i>, <i>FIL2</i>, and the F<sub>2</sub> mapping population were exposed to a short term recovery drought experiment following Lovell et al.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Lovell, J. T. et al. Drought responsive gene expression regulatory divergence between upland and lowland ecotypes of a perennial C4 grass. Genome Res. 26, 510–518 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-018-07669-x#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d12848e3188">44</a></sup>. In short, all plants experienced a natural 30-day drought at the Ladybird Johnson Wildflower Center (Austin, TX; 30.19° N, 97.87° W). Drought treatment plants were harvested on 5 July 2013, while recovery treatment plants received 4 L of water on 7 July 2013 and were harvested on 8 July 2013. For each plant, we measured midday leaf water potential (LWP, Ψ<sub>leaf</sub>) with a Scholander-type pressure bomb (PMS Instruments, model 1000) between 11:00 and 13:00. All plants reached anthesis by 5 July 2013.</p><p>Leaf tissue harvest was conducted on 5 and 8 July 2013 between 11:00 and 13:00, where the most recent fully emerged leaf was immediately flash frozen with liquid nitrogen. For total gene expression assays, RNA (3 µg, RIN ≥ 5) was extracted from 50–200 mg of homogenized (Geno/Grinder, Spex SamplePrep) and DNase 1-treated leaf tissue with RNeasy Plant Mini kits (Qiagen). Total RNA libraries were prepared on a PerkinElmer SciClone NGS robotic liquid handling system using Illumina’s TruSeq Stranded mRNA HT Sample Prep kit with 1 µg RNA per sample, and 10 cycles of library amplification PCR. Library quantification by KAPA Biosystem’s next-generation sequencing library qPCR was accomplished on a Roche LightCycler 480 real-time PCR instrument. Sequencing was performed on the Illumina HiSeq 2000 sequencer and a TruSeq SBS sequencing kit (200 cycles, v3, following a 2 × 150 indexed run recipe).</p><p>To quantify expression, we mapped reads to concatenated genome assemblies and annotations of the <i>HAL2</i> and <i>FIL2</i> genomes. Uniquely mapping reads were counted with STAR<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Dobin, A. et al. STAR: ultrafast universal RNA-seq aligner. Bioinformatics 29, 15–21 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-018-07669-x#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d12848e3206">45</a></sup> – by definition these reads represent allele-specific expression (ASE). Shared (not allele-specific) reads were those that mapped to both orthologs; these were counted with the subread<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Liao, Y., Smyth, G. K. &amp; Shi, W. The Subread aligner: fast, accurate and scalable read mapping by seed-and-vote. Nucleic Acids Res. 41, e108–e108 (2013)." href="/articles/s41467-018-07669-x#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d12848e3210">46</a></sup> featureCount function. Total counts, calculated as the sum of ASE and shared counts, were used for eQTL analyses. High-confidence gene models with reciprocal best hit one-to-one orthologs and average total counts ≥1 were retained for further analysis. Total counts were voom<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Ritchie, M. E. et al. limma powers differential expression analyses for RNA-sequencing and microarray studies. Nucleic Acids Res. 43, e47–e47 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-018-07669-x#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d12848e3214">23</a></sup> normalized for eQTL analysis.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec14">eQTL analysis</h3><p>Expression quantitative locus mapping was performed in R/qtl<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Broman, K. W., Wu, H., Sen, S. &amp; Churchill, G. A. R/qtl: QTL mapping in experimental crosses.
