Bioinformatics 19, 889–890 (2003)." href="/articles/s41467-018-07669-x#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d12848e3226">47</a></sup>. It is common to perform eQTL via one-way single QTL scans for each gene expression trait. However, there are several multi-locus correlated regions in our genetic map, where markers on one chromosome are in linkage disequilibrium (LD) with markers on another chromosome (Supplementary Figure <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-07669-x#MOESM1">7</a>). Therefore, genes with cis-eQTL that are physically within these LD regions may have spurious trans-eQTL on other chromosomes. Since previous results suggested that cis-eQTL were pervasive and of large effect in <i>HAL2</i>-<i>FIL2</i> crosses<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Lovell, J. T. et al. Drought responsive gene expression regulatory divergence between upland and lowland ecotypes of a perennial C4 grass. Genome Res. 26, 510–518 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-018-07669-x#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d12848e3239">44</a></sup>, we scanned for QTL peaks, conditioning on the additive covariates of treatment and the cis-eQTL genotype.</p><p>Both additive and QTL-by-treatment (GxE eQTL) scans were accomplished using the Haley-Knott algorithm on Hidden Markov Model multipoint genotype probabilities. The lowest genome-wide error rate-controlled empirical <i>P</i>-value (1000 permutations) for each transcript abundance phenotype was multiple-test corrected using the <i>Q</i>-value approach<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Storey, J. D. A direct approach to false discovery rates. J. R. Stat. Soc. B 64, 479–498 (2002)." href="/articles/s41467-018-07669-x#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d12848e3252">48</a></sup>. The maximum significant (<i>α</i> = 0.05) empirical <i>P</i>-value was enforced as the trans-significance threshold. Only trans-eQTL LOD peaks that were more significant than this threshold were retained. Since cis-eQTL were not subject to genomic scans, we employed a standard <i>Q</i>-value transformed ANOVA <i>χ</i><sup>2</sup><i>P</i>-value threshold, retaining cis effects with FDR-corrected <i>P</i>-values ≤0.05. Both cis- and trans-GxE effects were determined by comparing the additive and full models via likelihood ratio tests. The resultant <i>P</i>-values were FDR corrected in an identical manner as the additive cis-eQTL test. Finally, we dropped any cis- or trans-eQTL with maximum LOD scores &lt;2.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec15">Genetic map construction</h3><p>To construct a linkage map, we calculated the ratio of <i>HAL2</i>/(<i>HAL2</i> + <i>FIL2</i>) allele-specific expression (ASE) for 15,082 gene models with &gt;5 ASE counts in each parent. For each chromosome and F<sub>2</sub> genotype, we conducted a 20-gene overlapping sliding window calculation of the proportion of <i>HAL2</i>-ASE. We expected clear tri-modality of <i>HAL2</i> allele frequency within each window, where the <i>HAL2</i> homozygotes were ~100% <i>HAL2</i> alleles, the heterozygotes were ~50%, and the <i>FIL2</i> homozygotes were ~0%. Given the F<sub>2</sub> population, the three peaks should be in approximately a 1:2:1 segregation ratio. Windows that did not conform to these expectations were dropped from the analysis.</p><p>To make calls, we first needed to develop thresholds that distinguished the three genotypes. Since the relative bias of allele frequency towards either parent in the heterozygote varied based on the markers in the window, a static threshold was not appropriate. Instead, we applied a dynamic threshold that separated the trimodal distribution of alleles into three bins. This dynamic thresholding eliminated library-specific biases towards either parent. The most common call (majority vote) in each 20-gene window was used as the genotype call. Calls were concatenated across libraries and chromosomes to produce a single genotype matrix.</p><p>Sliding window majority vote markers were processed to produce a saturated and non-redundant genetic map. Markers were first clustered into groups with identical genotypes. The marker with the least missing data was retained for each cluster of identical markers, resulting in a matrix of 3555 unique markers. We then clustered pairs of markers with ≤0.005 recombination fractions, retaining the marker that minimized missing data and segregation distortion. The physical assembly position was used to order markers and form linkage groups. After estimating a genetic map with the Kosambi mapping function<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Broman, K. W., Wu, H., Sen, S. &amp; Churchill, G. A. R/qtl: QTL mapping in experimental crosses. Bioinformatics 19, 889–890 (2003)." href="/articles/s41467-018-07669-x#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d12848e3322">47</a></sup>, we culled markers within 0.5 cM, retaining the marker with lower segregation distortion. Finally, we tested for potentially problematic markers by assessing the genetic map size before and after dropping each marker. Those markers that resulted in an expansion of the map &gt;1 cM were culled. The final map contained 1519 markers.</p><p>Given the approach of genotyping via allele-specific expression counts, we sought to test the genotyping error. To accomplish this, we assumed the marker order was correct, and iteratively checked parameters of map expansion across a range of assumed genotyping errors<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Broman, K. Genetic map construction with R/qtl. biostat.wisc.edu. (2010).&#xA;                        " href="/articles/s41467-018-07669-x#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d12848e3329">49</a></sup>. For all chromosomes, the −log likelihood of the genetic map was maximized at 1 × 10<sup>−6</sup> (Supplementary Figure <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-07669-x#MOESM1">8</a>), indicating that we would expect ≤1 genotyping error among all 200,430 marker-by-individual combinations in our genetic map.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec16">Tests for selection on eQTL</h3><p>To test for selection on the evolution of gene expression, we employed a sign test where the odds ratio of cis- and trans- up- and down-regulatory evolution was tested via a Fisher’s exact test. Directional effects of each QTL were calculated using the fitqtl function in R/qtl. Estimates of QTL effects were calculated as the <i>t</i>-statistic of replacing the <i>HAL2</i> allele with the <i>FIL2</i> allele at a given locus. Deviations from the expected odds ratio (1:1) imply non-neutral evolution<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Fraser, H. B., Moses, A. M. &amp; Schadt, E. E. Evidence for widespread adaptive evolution of gene expression in budding yeast. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 107, 2977–2982 (2010)." href="/articles/s41467-018-07669-x#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d12848e3355">29</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="Artieri, C. G. &amp; Fraser, H. B. Evolution at two levels of gene expression in yeast. Genome Res. 24, 411–421 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-018-07669-x#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d12848e3358">50</a></sup>. We also tested the odds ratio against the overall prevalence of antagonistic and reinforcing effects across the genome. This comparison allowed for inference of outlier regions relative to the genome average of cis-trans allelic effects.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec17">Promoter and coding DNA sequence variant annotation</h3><p>Reciprocal best hit (RBH) orthologs are defined as those pairs of genes where each is the other best matching sequence when compared to the alternate genome. Here we tested for 1:1 RBH orthologs by finding the best <i>HAL2</i>(query):<i>FIL2</i>(target) and <i>FIL2</i>(query):<i>HAL2</i>(target) protein BLAT<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Kent, W. J. BLAT--the BLAST-like alignment tool. Genome Res. 12, 656–664 (2002)." href="/articles/s41467-018-07669-x#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d12848e3382">43</a></sup> scores. The genes where both BLAT runs produced the same set of maximal gene pairings were the initial set of RBH orthologs. Given the known high degree of synteny, we then culled this set to include only pairings where both genes were on the same chromosomal region, allowing for 20% of the total chromosome length as buffer.</p><p>To annotate coding DNA sequences (CDS), <i>FIL2</i> CDS sequences were aligned against corresponding <i>HAL2</i> sequences using Minimap2<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Li, H. Minimap2: pairwise alignment for nucleotide sequences. Bioinformatics 3, 321 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-018-07669-x#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d12848e3395">51</a></sup>. The resulting alignment file was subset to 26786 <i>HAL2</i>-<i>FIL2</i> orthologs. A pileup-formatted file was generated using SAMtools<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Li, H. et al. The sequence Alignment/Map format and SAMtools. Bioinformatics 25, 2078–2079 (2009)." href="/articles/s41467-018-07669-x#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d12848e3406">52</a></sup> mpileup utility. VarScan2<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 53" title="Koboldt, D. C. et al. VarScan 2: somatic mutation and copy number alteration discovery in cancer by exome sequencing. Genome Res. 22, 568–576 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-018-07669-x#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d12848e3410">53</a></sup> was used to call variants (SNPs and INDELs). Those variants were then annotated using SnpEFF<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="Cingolani, P. et al. A program for annotating and predicting the effects of single nucleotide polymorphisms, SnpEff. Fly 6, 80–92 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-018-07669-x#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d12848e3414">54</a></sup>. SNP and INDEL variant annotations were described as synonymous, moderate effect (e.g. missense mutation), high effect (e.g. premature stop), or insertion/deletion. All genes were classified as either having or lacking significant CDS evolution. Significant genes had at least one moderate or larger effect SNP or INDEL, while non-significant genes were monomorphic, or had only low effect variants.</p><p>We annotated transcription factor binding affinity (TFBA) of the promoter sequences (defined as 2 kb upstream of the transcriptional start site) by extracting and comparing relative binding affinity of a set of 35 transcription factor families. Transcription factor binding sites (motifs) were downloaded from the JASPAR2016 plant database<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 55" title="Mathelier, A. et al. JASPAR 2016: a major expansion and update of the open-access database of transcription factor binding profiles. Nucleic Acids Res. 44, D110–D115 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-018-07669-x#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d12848e3421">55</a></sup> as positional frequency matrices. Affinity of each motif was scored for each promoter sequence and differential binding affinity was tested using the R package PWMEnrich<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 56" title="Stojnic, R. &amp; Diez, D. PWMEnrich: PWM enrichment analysis. (R package version, 2014)." href="/articles/s41467-018-07669-x#ref-CR56" id="ref-link-section-d12848e3425">56</a></sup>. Binomial tests for equal affinity were performed for each gene and transcription factor. Significance was determined by controlling for global false discovery rate at <i>α</i> = 0.05 by transforming the binomial <i>P</i>-values using the Benjami-Hochberg method. All genes were classified as either having or lacking significant TFBA evolution. Significant genes had at least one transcription factor with a significant binomial test while all transcription factors in non-significant TFBA genes had FDR-corrected binomial <i>P</i>-values ≥0.05.</p><p>In many cases, we sought to compare the relative prevalence of genes with and without TFBA and CDS evolution. To accomplish this, we employed a Fisher’s exact test, reporting the odds ratio and exact <i>P</i>-value.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec18">Population genetic analyses</h3><p>We re-sequenced 94 <i>P. hallii</i> genotypes; 78 <i>var. hallii</i>, and 15 <i>var. filipes</i>. We selected more <i>hallii</i> samples than <i>filipes</i> as the geographic range of <i>hallii</i> is far larger than that of <i>filipes</i>. If identical sample sizes were used, we would be comparing geographically proximate <i>filipes</i> and geographically distant <i>hallii</i>. Since there is at least some isolation-by-distance in <i>P. hallii</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Lowry, D. B., Purmal, C. T. &amp; Juenger, T. E. A population genetic transect of Panicum hallii (Poaceae). Am. J. Bot. 100, 592–601 (2013)." href="/articles/s41467-018-07669-x#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d12848e3482">16</a></sup>, we instead opted to choose sample sizes that generally reflect the relative range size of each variety.</p><p>Illumina HiSeq2500 2 × 150 reads were mapped to the <i>HAL2</i> genome reference via bwa-mem<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 57" title="Li, H. &amp; Durbin, R. Fast and accurate short read alignment with Burrows-Wheeler transform. Bioinformatics 25, 1754–1760 (2009)." href="/articles/s41467-018-07669-x#ref-CR57" id="ref-link-section-d12848e3492">57</a></sup>, followed by samtools<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Li, H. et al. The sequence Alignment/Map format and SAMtools. Bioinformatics 25, 2078–2079 (2009)." href="/articles/s41467-018-07669-x#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d12848e3496">52</a></sup> to sort the bam and create a multisample mileup<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Li, H. et al. The sequence Alignment/Map format and SAMtools. Bioinformatics 25, 2078–2079 (2009)." href="/articles/s41467-018-07669-x#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d12848e3500">52</a></sup>. Single nucleotide polymorphisms (SNP) and insertion-deletion (indel) polymorphisms were called via the Varscan 2.4.0<sup>53</sup> pipeline as above, with a minimum coverage of 8, and minimum reads of 4. A binomial test was used to confirm the allelic state (homozygous or heterozygous) at a <i>P</i>-value of 0.05. SNPs within 25 bp of a repeat were removed. A minimum presence of allele in 90% samples was required for further analysis.</p><p>Population structure was estimated using fastStructure<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 58" title="Raj, A., Stephens, M. &amp; Pritchard, J. K. fastSTRUCTURE: variational inference of population structure in large SNP data sets. Genetics 197, 573–589 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-018-07669-x#ref-CR58" id="ref-link-section-d12848e3513">58</a></sup> and TESS<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 59" title="Caye, K., Deist, T. M., Martins, H., Michel, O. &amp; Francois, O. TESS3: fast inference of spatial population structure and genome scans for selection. Mol. Ecol. Resour. 16, 540–548 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-018-07669-x#ref-CR59" id="ref-link-section-d12848e3517">59</a></sup> with a subset of 50k markers, that were LD pruned (parameters: --indep-pairwise 50 50 0.5) in plink<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 60" title="Purcell, S. et al. PLINK: a tool set for whole-genome association and population-based linkage analyses. Am. J. Human Genet. 81, 559–575 (2007)." href="/articles/s41467-018-07669-x#ref-CR60" id="ref-link-section-d12848e3521">60</a></sup>. A single sample with a membership coefficient (qi) of &lt;0.7 was considered admixed.</p><p>To infer demographic history, samples were phased (via SHAPEIT<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Schiffels, S. &amp; Durbin, R. Inferring human population size and separation history from multiple genome sequences. Nat. Genet. 46, 919–925 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-018-07669-x#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d12848e3528">17</a></sup>). Sites were filtered for unique 24mers to minimize spurious variant calls. These data were input into MSMC2<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Schiffels, S. &amp; Durbin, R. Inferring human population size and separation history from multiple genome sequences. Nat. Genet. 46, 919–925 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-018-07669-x#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d12848e3532">17</a></sup> to estimate effective population size and divergence using the following parameters: 4 haplotypes for each subpopulation, skipping ambiguous sites and a time segment pattern = ‘10*2 + 20*5 + 10*2’ and an estimated rhoOverMu of 0.25. We estimated rhoOverMu as 0.25 as the mean value from 100 iterations without the fixed recombination parameter of five sets of four haplotypes in each subpopulation and averaged them. To estimate scaled times were converted to years assuming a generation time of 1 year and a mutation rate of 6.5*10–8. 50 bootstrap datasets obtained using MSMC tools, and simulations using the same parameters above were performed for each of the estimate to get the confidence intervals for population size estimates.</p><p>For the phylogenetic analysis, we first defined syntenic blocks to obtain an outgroup for the analysis. To define syntenic blocks, CDS DNA sequences were pulled and reciprocally mapped via blat<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Kent, W. J. BLAT--the BLAST-like alignment tool. Genome Res. 12, 656–664 (2002)." href="/articles/s41467-018-07669-x#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d12848e3540">43</a></sup> between the <i>P. hallii</i> genomes, <i>Setaria viridis</i>, <i>Sorghum bicolor</i>, and Switchgrass. Mappings with scores &gt;100 and within 50% of the maximum mapping score for each gene were retained. Two-dimensional k-nearest neighbor clustering was employed to cull mappings to regions of dense hits in both genomes. For each chromosome pair, we chained mappings together using a traveling salesperson problem solver, which can be used to infer marker order in genetic mapping<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 61" title="Monroe, J. G. et al. TSPmap, a tool making use of traveling salesperson problem solvers in the efficient and accurate construction of high-density genetic linkage maps. BioData Min. 10, 1305 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-018-07669-x#ref-CR61" id="ref-link-section-d12848e3553">61</a></sup>. These chains were split if gaps &gt;500 kb existed, and re-chained if two blocks were adjacent. We parsed 30 large syntenic blocks, which contained 326k variable sites across all non-admixed libraries. A random set of 50 K SNP were used for ML tree construction in RAXML<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 62" title="Stamatakis, A. RAxML version 8: a tool for phylogenetic analysis and post-analysis of large phylogenies. Bioinformatics 30, btu033–1313 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-018-07669-x#ref-CR62" id="ref-link-section-d12848e3558">62</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec19">Dating divergence time</h3><p>OrthoFinder<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Emms, D. M. &amp; Kelly, S. OrthoFinder: solving fundamental biases in whole genome comparisons dramatically improves orthogroup inference accuracy. Genome Biol. 16, E9 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-018-07669-x#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d12848e3570">41</a></sup> was used with default parameters to identify clusters of single copy genes in 7 species along with <i>Arabidopsis</i> as outgroup. Of the 3311 ortholog sets, we randomly chose 100 sets and aligned them in Clustal-omega<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 63" title="Sievers, F. &amp; Higgins, D. G. Clustal Omega, accurate alignment of very large numbers of sequences. Methods Mol. Biol. 1079, 105–116 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-018-07669-x#ref-CR63" id="ref-link-section-d12848e3577">63</a></sup>. We used a HKY substitution model with 4 gamma rate categories and an uncorrelated log-normal relaxed clock model in BEAST2 (v2.4.7)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 64" title="Bouckaert, R. et al. BEAST 2: a software platform for bayesian evolutionary analysis. PLoS Comput. Biol. 10, e1003537 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-018-07669-x#ref-CR64" id="ref-link-section-d12848e3581">64</a></sup> to estimate the divergence times. A calibration time of 52 Mya was used for <i>Brachypodium</i> and <i>Sorghum</i> split. We performed four independent runs, each with 10 million burn-ins and 50 million chain length, saving every 5000 chains. LogCombiner<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 64" title="Bouckaert, R. et al. BEAST 2: a software platform for bayesian evolutionary analysis. PLoS Comput. Biol. 10, e1003537 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-018-07669-x#ref-CR64" id="ref-link-section-d12848e3592">64</a></sup> was used to combine trees that have an effective sample size of at least 200. The consensus trees were further estimated in TreeAnnotator<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 64" title="Bouckaert, R. et al. BEAST 2: a software platform for bayesian evolutionary analysis. PLoS Comput. Biol. 10, e1003537 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-018-07669-x#ref-CR64" id="ref-link-section-d12848e3596">64</a></sup> using mean and median node heights.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec20">qPCR validation of RNA sequencing</h3><p>For quantitative RT-PCR, we isolated total RNA from the leaves using an RNA extraction kit (TRIzol reagent, Invitrogen). About 1 μg total RNA was reverse-transcribed using SSII reverse transcriptase (Invitrogen) in a volume of 80 μl to obtain cDNA. We used primers <i>PhABO3q</i>-F (5′-CCGCATACTGGATCTCACAA-3′) and <i>PhABO3q</i>-R (5′-TCTGAATCCAGTGGCACATC-3′) for amplifying the transcript of <i>PhABO3</i> and <i>PhUBIq</i>-F (5′-TCATTTCGATGCTCTCTAGTCC-3′) and <i>PhUBIq</i>-R (5′-TTCCTGAGATTTGCGAACAATG-3′) for ubiquitin-conjugating enzyme (<i>Pahal.3G250900</i>) as the internal control. We carried out quantitative RT-PCR in a total volume of 10 μl containing 2 μl of the reverse-transcribed product above, 0.25 μM gene-specific primers and 5 μl LightCycler 480 SYBR Green I Master (Roche) on a Roche LightCycler 480 II real-time PCR System according to the manufacturer’s instructions. The measurements were obtained using the relative quantification method<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 65" title="Livak, K. J., &amp; Schmittgen, T. D. Analysis of relative gene expression data using real-time quantitative PCR and the 2− ΔΔCT method. Methods 25, 402–408 (2001)." href="/articles/s41467-018-07669-x#ref-CR65" id="ref-link-section-d12848e3627">65</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec21">Recombinant inbred line field experiment</h3><p>294 F<sub>7</sub> individuals of the <i>HAL2</i>x<i>FIL2</i> recombinant inbred line (RIL) population<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Khasanova, A. et al. The genetic architecture of shoot and root trait divergence between upland and lowland ecotypes of a perennial grass. Preprint at &#xA;                    https://doi.org/10.1101/301531&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-018-07669-x#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d12848e3648">34</a></sup> were grown at Brackenridge Field Laboratory (BFL, Austin, TX, USA) in spring and summer of 2016. Seeds were germinated in the BFL greenhouse on 29-Feburary and 3-March 2016. For each RIL line, seed coats for 40 seed were removed by scarification, and seeds were germinated in wet aquarium sand on petri plates. Sand was rewetted as necessary with Miracle Gro liquid fertilizer mix. Plate locations were randomly cycled daily in the greenhouse to standardize growth conditions. Germination occurred between 3–5 March and on 7–10 March. The healthiest seedlings were transplanted from sand to 32-cell trays to generate at least eight healthy replicates per line. Transplants were thinned to the most robust individual per cell between 23–29 March, and 294 lines with eight healthy individuals were selected for inclusion in the field design. On 7 April, plants were randomized within trays into the field experiment layout. To standardize growing conditions, trays were also cycled once in the greenhouse between transplanting and randomization.</p><p>RIL individuals were transplanted in the field on 14–15 April in 32 interspersed wet and dry treatment beds, three individuals wide and 26 individuals long. These blocks were surrounded by a border row of <i>FIL2</i> individuals. Individuals were spaced by 0.5 m along the length of the bed, and by 1.25 m along the width of the bed and between beds. Four individuals from each RIL line were randomized into both the wet treatment and the dry treatment beds to make a randomized split-plot design. Transplanting survival was very high, but nine plants were replaced in mid-May due to transplanting death. The entire field was irrigated on 14, 15, and 20 April to reduce transplantation shock. Thereafter, the wet treatment beds were frequently irrigated between 17-June and 8-August in order to achieve &gt;50% soil volumetric water content. The dry treatment beds were irrigated at roughly 1/5th the frequency, at a level sufficient to achieve 30% soil volumetric water content, as measured with a Campbell Scientific CWS655 soil moisture probe. We measured two phenotypes: leaf relative water content (RWC = (weight<sub>fresh</sub>−wt<sub>dry</sub>)/(wt<sub>turgid</sub>−wt<sub>dry</sub>)) and leaf chlorophyll content (SPAD, using a SPAD 502 chlorophyll meter). Phenotypic tissue harvest and measurements took place from 11–15 July 2016 between 9AM and 1PM.</p><p>The best linear unbiased predictors were calculated for each genotype-by-treatment combination, where bed was treated as a random effect in the split plot design. The maximal position of each trans-eQTL hotspot was extracted and converted from the <i>HAL2</i> coordinate system of the F<sub>2</sub> genetic map to the <i>FIL2</i> (v2.0) coordinate system of the RIL genetic map<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Khasanova, A. et al. The genetic architecture of shoot and root trait divergence between upland and lowland ecotypes of a perennial grass. Preprint at &#xA;                    https://doi.org/10.1101/301531&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-018-07669-x#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d12848e3677">34</a></sup>. The multipoint genotype probability for the proximate RIL marker to each hotspot was inferred via a hidden Markov model, as implemented in R/qtl’s calc.genoprob function. Finally, we applied single marker tests for marker-phenotype associations between RWC and SPAD in the three conditions (wet, dry, plasticity [wet-dry]) as implemented in R/qtl’s fitqtl function.</p></div></div></section>
                

                <section aria-labelledby="data-availability" data-title="Data availability"><div class="c-article-section" id="data-availability-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="data-availability">Data availability</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="data-availability-content">
              
              <p>All RNA and resequencing reads have been deposited in the NCBI Short Read Archive (<a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/sra">https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/sra</a>). Bioprojects, sample IDs, and metadata can be found in Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-07669-x#MOESM4">1</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-07669-x#MOESM10">7</a>. Both genome assemblies and annotations are available through phytozome (<a href="https://phytozome.jgi.doe.gov">https://phytozome.jgi.doe.gov</a>). The assemblies have also been deposited on Genbank (<a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/genbank">https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/genbank</a>) under BioProjects <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/bioproject/251785">PRJNA251785</a> (<i>HAL2</i>) and <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/bioproject/250527">PRJNA250527</a> (<i>FIL2</i>). All statistical, QTL mapping, and visualization functions were implemented in R 3.4.3 and have been compiled into an R package stored on github [<a href="https://github.com/jtlovell/qtlTools">https://github.com/jtlovell/qtlTools</a>]. Additional supporting data is provided as Supplementary Data. Details are provided in the Description of Additional Supplementary Files and the Reporting Summary. The source data underlying Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-07669-x#MOESM10">1</a>A-B, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-07669-x#MOESM7">2</a>A-D, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-07669-x#MOESM10">3</a>B-C and Supplementary Figure <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-07669-x#MOESM1">3</a>–<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-07669-x#MOESM1">7</a> are provided as a Source Data File. Source data for Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-07669-x#MOESM7">3</a>A, D and Supplementary Figure <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-07669-x#MOESM1">1</a>–<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-07669-x#MOESM1">2</a> are found in Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-07669-x#MOESM10">7</a> and Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-07669-x#MOESM5">2</a>, respectively. A reporting summary for this Article is available as a Supplementary <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-07669-x#MOESM1">Information</a> file.</p>

Heiling, A. Asmus, C. Purmal, D. Des Marais, J. Reilley, T.S. Quedensley, B. Whitaker, K. Hernandez, E. Milano, M.J. Aspinwall, S.H. Taylor, and other members of the Juenger lab at UT Austin. We thank the UT Brackenridge Field Lab and the Ladybird Johnson Wildflower Center for providing support and field sites for experiments. Y. Stuart, S. Sen, K. Broman, D. Hoover, R. Williamson, R. Holmer, E. Schranz, and D. Bolnick provided advice on methods and presentation. Computational analyses were conducted, in part, on the Stampede system with allocations from the Texas Advance Computing Center. We thank Dan Sisco and Bill LaRochelle from 454/Roche for early data contributions to the <i>filipes</i> reference and Michael Kertesz for early access to Moleculo library technology. We thank collaborators at the Donald Danforth Center and the Joint Genome Institute for access to the prepublication genome and annotation of <i>Seteria viridis</i> to use in the outgroup analysis.
